    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7906396324992140981">Esta obra de Miguel de Unamuno es uno de los ejemplos clásicos más eminentes de la novela moderna. La ficción deja aquí de ser un puro vehículo narrativo, transmisor de historias, para convertirse en un universo textual de fecundas sugerencias. El título, <i>Niebla</i>, expresa con claridad el propósito novelesco de desdibujar lo visible y materializar, en cambio, lo impalpable. </span>
  <span id="freeText7906396324992140981" style="display:none">Esta obra de Miguel de Unamuno es uno de los ejemplos clásicos más eminentes de la novela moderna. La ficción deja aquí de ser un puro vehículo narrativo, transmisor de historias, para convertirse en un universo textual de fecundas sugerencias. El título, <i>Niebla</i>, expresa con claridad el propósito novelesco de desdibujar lo visible y materializar, en cambio, lo impalpable. En este ambiente vemos moverse a un hombre esencialmente frustrado, Augusto Pérez, sobre cuya muerte nos vemos obligados a pronunciarnos. Germán Gullón, reconocido como uno de los primeros especialistas en novela contemporánea, facilita en esta edición una pauta de lectura que conduce a una riqueza de comprensión insospechada de la novela en sí y de cuanto supone, como cima, en el proceso de la narrativa española.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7906396324992140981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div id="review_814291642" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/814291642" />
    <a title="Kalliope" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope"><img alt="Kalliope" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1428482885p2/3593962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/814291642?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kalliope" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kalliope" href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope">Kalliope</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=spain">spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3593962-kalliope?shelf=fiction-spanish">fiction-spanish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer814291642" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3156972037823639449"><br/>I am surprised this novel is not better known outside Spain. Or may be I am not.<br/><br/>Niebla, or Mist, was published in 1914 but it was written considerably earlier, in 1907. Consequently, it ranks amongst the earliest modernist novels, on a par with Pirandello’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3071706.Il_fu_Mattia_Pascal' title='Il fu Mattia Pascal by Luigi Pirandello' rel='nofollow noopener'>Il fu Mattia Pascal</a> (from 1904 and which I have not read - yet) and with Proust’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/201497.Du_c_t__de_chez_Swann____la_recherche_du_temps_perdu___1_' title='Du côté de chez Swann (À la recherche du temps perdu, #1) by Marcel Proust' rel='nofollow noopener'>Du côté de chez Swann</a> (published in 1913 but also written from 1909 onwards).<br/><br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1391960812i/8480971.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Don Miguel de Unamuno (1864-1939) is associated to what is known as the “98 Generat</span>
  <span id="freeText3156972037823639449" style="display:none"><br>I am surprised this novel is not better known outside Spain. Or may be I am not.<br><br>Niebla, or Mist, was published in 1914 but it was written considerably earlier, in 1907. Consequently, it ranks amongst the earliest modernist novels, on a par with Pirandello’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3071706.Il_fu_Mattia_Pascal" title="Il fu Mattia Pascal by Luigi Pirandello" rel="nofollow noopener">Il fu Mattia Pascal</a> (from 1904 and which I have not read - yet) and with Proust’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/201497.Du_c_t__de_chez_Swann____la_recherche_du_temps_perdu___1_" title="Du côté de chez Swann (À la recherche du temps perdu, #1) by Marcel Proust" rel="nofollow noopener">Du côté de chez Swann</a> (published in 1913 but also written from 1909 onwards).<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1391960812i/8480971.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Don Miguel de Unamuno (1864-1939) is associated to what is known as the “98 Generation” (<i>Generación del 98</i>), or a group of writers and thinkers, who with a relatively international outlook, were marked by a pessimism that resulted from their realization of the serious decline of Spain. The year of 1898, when Spain lost its last colonies, Cuba and the Philippines, became their identity mark. They formed a group with shared friendships and allegiances and a general interest in new literary forms. <br><br>This mist of a novel is not so misty. The language is crystal clear and reads as easily as a soft breeze blows through one’s hair. Its supposed modernist quality is, however, a bit more obscure. Or may be it is not. <br><br>The title, Niebla, is a play of words with the word Novela (novel) and a term that Unamuno coined for a new literary genre of his own cooking: <b>"Nívola”</b>. Niebla therefore does not refer to the plot, and even if the word crops up regularly and playfully in the novel, it refers indirectly to what Unamuno believed a novel should be. This is then his nebulous experiment. <br><br>Reading Niebla we follow Unamuno’s somewhat foolish character <i>Don Augusto Pérez</i> and the silly plot as he questions the premises of the realist novel. His questioning is also rooted in his fascination with the work by the other Don Miguel of Spanish literature, and Don Quijote makes its presence in Niebla both through the invocation of the “ingenioso hidalgo” and through some of the literary characteristics of the work.<br><br>In this Nívola of Niebla, all the modernist ingredients are there: clearly spelled out.<br><br>Unamuno’s first concern is with the principle of cohesive and ordered narrative and with the structure of the novel. As an alternative he proposes to let the novel write itself, without plan. The plot is to unfold in a muddled and disorderly fashion, similarly to life. The characters will form themselves through their use of language, through what they say, but in a loose and pointless talk (or “hablar por hablar”). They will speak a great deal even if they have nothing to say but this language will portray them. There is consequently a great deal of dialogue, or the most natural of spontaneous of languages, in Niebla. This writing without a goal is however, at a great distance from the later Surrealist "automatic writing".<br><br>Niebla then reads very similarly to a play. There is no Modernist twisting of meanings. Instead, the emptying of human utterance and the emphasis on its banality underlines Unamuno’s concern about the theatricality and falsehood of speech. Language in its social aspect becomes suspect. Alternatively, he also wants to explore the inner language and how to get into the mind of the protagonist without making the author too impossibly intrusive. Rather than a Woolfian stream of consciousness, we have a dog. This is a charming dog. The suitably named Orfeo becomes the Alter-Ego of Don Augusto, so that we know what he thinks, again through dialogue, and at the end the dog will also utter his own words. This literary role of the dog is explicitly explained to us by Unamuno, who increasingly interferes in his novel.<br><br>There are more modernist characteristics in this Nívola. Musings on Time and its meaning are often encountered (<i>Y así, sin término, devanando la madeja de nuestro destino, deshaciendo todo el infinito que en una eternidad nos ha hecho, caminando a la nada, sin llegar nunca a ella, pues que ella nunca fue</i>). Identity and its definition or fragmentation is also found, even if Don Augusto’s paltry insistence on his “<i>Yo soy yo</i>”, or ”<i>el otro soy yo</i>” and “<i>si, yo soy el otro, yo soy otro</i>” just made me laugh.<br><br>But the most striking aspect of this novel is Unamuno playing with himself and his Authorship. It all begins with the fictitious Prologue of a fictitious writer who disagrees with the real author on what is the true plot of the novel. The author will then later on creep in the novel to tell us that his characters are talking about him and rebelling against him, but he laughs at their futile efforts, since he is the god of this literary creation (<i>Yo soy el Dios de estos dos pobres diablos nivolescos</i>). And the novel finishes with the argument between the protagonist and the author on how the Nívola, and Don Augusto’s life, should end. Who do you think got the upper hand?<br><br>At the beginning of my reading, the clarity and simplicity in the writing disappointed me. I felt a divergence between the intentions and the form, but as I proceeded, I detected more and more of the humour and laughed out loud several times.<br><br>I applaud Unamuno in his ability to formulate a parody of something which was not quite there yet.<br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1391960812i/8480972._SY540_.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="3156972037823639449" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1997981159" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1997981159" />
    <a title="Fran " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12653120-fran"><img alt="Fran " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1473715052p2/12653120.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1997981159?book_show_action=true">Jun 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fran " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fran " href="/user/show/12653120-fran">Fran </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780810135376" href="/book/show/34890246-fog">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1997981159" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16099045772912697622">1914. Politically unsettling times at the onset of The Great War. Fragmentation in the world and a tendency to see through a glass darkly perhaps was the inspiration for "Fog" written by Miguel de Unamuno.<br/><br/>Augusto, a pampered, wealthy intellectual, still reeling two years after his mother's death, was bored with life. Bored, until the day he spotted a girl with beautiful eyes. He followed her home and gleaned the necessary information. She was single, orphaned, and lived with her aunt and uncle. </span>
  <span id="freeText16099045772912697622" style="display:none">1914. Politically unsettling times at the onset of The Great War. Fragmentation in the world and a tendency to see through a glass darkly perhaps was the inspiration for "Fog" written by Miguel de Unamuno.<br /><br />Augusto, a pampered, wealthy intellectual, still reeling two years after his mother's death, was bored with life. Bored, until the day he spotted a girl with beautiful eyes. He followed her home and gleaned the necessary information. She was single, orphaned, and lived with her aunt and uncle. Left almost destitute by her father after a stock market crash, she gave piano lessons to pay off the heavily mortgaged house she had inherited. Up until now, Augusto existed in a fog. He now had wild dreams about the undying love of his own creation.<br /><br />Augusto, introverted and unsure of himself, confers with friend Victor. Victor tells him that Eugenia's presence has awakened Augusto's senses to women collectively. Feelings for a singular woman will help him to consider branching out and experiencing life and love. Augusto's "over the top" fascination with Eugenia is problematic since Eugenia has a fiance, Mauricio. Despite Mauricio's presence, Augusto is selfless. He pays off Eugenia's mortgage and eliminates her debts.<br /><br />While Eugenia's aunt calls Augusto her favorite suitor, strong willed Eugenia is determined to marry lazy, unemployed Mauricio, a bum who detests the idea of work. She feels that Augusto is trying to "buy her".<br /><br />The fog that is life envelopes Augusto. He converses with his dog Orfeo, who listens to his soliloquies and thoughts on love. Augusto wants to end his own life. He visits author Unamuno, his creator. Unamuno informs him that he is just a character in a novel and only an author can make life or death decisions for the characters.<br /><br />"Fog" by Miguel de Unamuno is a tragic-comedy with existential themes. It is very creative and thought provoking. A most enjoyable tome.<br /><br />Thank you Northwestern University Press and Net Galley for the opportunity to read and review "Fog".</span>
  <a data-text-id="16099045772912697622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1564449719" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1564449719" />
    <a title="Simona B" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5312173-simona-b"><img alt="Simona B" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408893128p2/5312173.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1564449719?book_show_action=true">Feb 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simona B" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simona B" href="/user/show/5312173-simona-b">Simona B</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Pasta blanda 9788437603476" href="/book/show/45673.Niebla">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=1900">1900</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5312173-simona-b?shelf=in-spanish">in-spanish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1564449719" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5162390793409986370"><b>"¿No es acaso la liturgia de todas las religiones un modo de brezar el sueño de Dios y que no despierte y deje de soñarnos?"<br/><br/>"Is not the whole liturgy, of all religions, only a way perhaps of soothing God in His dreams, so that He shall not wake and cease to dream us?"</b><br/><br/><b>Mind-boggling concept</b>, and yet it manages to still seem <b>desolately average</b> -it's a miracle.<br/><br/>It may just be that in this period words seem to not want to come at me -they almost seem to refuse to be summoned, or maybe they want me me </span>
  <span id="freeText5162390793409986370" style="display:none"><b>"¿No es acaso la liturgia de todas las religiones un modo de brezar el sueño de Dios y que no despierte y deje de soñarnos?"<br /><br />"Is not the whole liturgy, of all religions, only a way perhaps of soothing God in His dreams, so that He shall not wake and cease to dream us?"</b><br /><br /><b>Mind-boggling concept</b>, and yet it manages to still seem <b>desolately average</b> -it's a miracle.<br /><br />It may just be that in this period words seem to not want to come at me -they almost seem to refuse to be summoned, or maybe they want me me to seek them harder, and I can't. So it may be me, it may be the words, but in this case I think it is the book. I don't feel like there is much about it to be said.<br /><br />You should probably know, before my attempt at <i>saying something</i>, that I am a huge admirer of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7702.Luigi_Pirandello" title="Luigi Pirandello" rel="nofollow noopener">Luigi Pirandello</a>'s work. To me, he is one of a kind, and he is unequalled. My Spanish professor swears up and down that Unamuno and Pirandello never read each other's works, but their philosophies are disconcertingly similar, and, useless to say, Unamuno's appears to me ad the rough copy of Pirandello's. This may be due exclusively to the fact that I'd read (and loved. And reread. And reloved) Pirandello before Unamuno's arrival in my literary panorama, or it may be due to the fact that Pirandello's world is so wide and deep and <i>ample</i> no matter the dimension you're considering, and Unamuno gave me not even the littlest hint of the <b>vertigo </b>Pirandello gives me. <br /><br />Now, moving on to the book: <i>Niebla</i> is <b>one of the most interesting literary experiments</b> I've ever had the pleasure to see. It's about the <b>collision</b> of two worlds, the real and the fictional ones, and this I can't help but enjoy. It's the story that the author uses as background where the main flaw of the book lies: uneventful, uninteresting, gray. <b>Un-meaningful </b>more than meaningless, and I can't say whether the latter would have been a less unappreciative adjective. Especially when reading books that I know have been written following a solid and sound conceptual system, I expect to and enjoy finding in each episode of the plot a more or less veiled reference to that system.<b> I like when actions are soaked with thoughts</b>. And what I felt was that in this book, if not very superficially, they weren't.<br /><br />I think I will just stick with Pirandello.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5162390793409986370" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2098560717" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2098560717" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2098560717?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810135369" href="/book/show/32072611-fog">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Michael by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3593962-kalliope">Kalliope</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=philosophical">philosophical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=dog">dog</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=spain">spain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2098560717" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5732033974895931688">A philosophical novel, recently republished, with a sufficient dose of the fun factor to make it a pleasure for me. Unamuno was a leftist novelist, playwright, and essayist who served as a professor of Greek and Classics at the University of Salamanca. Written in 1907 and published in 1914, “Niebla” (translated as mist or fog) purports through one of its characters to represent a new form of fiction, called the “nivola”, which is concerned more with the personification of ideas than realism. It </span>
  <span id="freeText5732033974895931688" style="display:none">A philosophical novel, recently republished, with a sufficient dose of the fun factor to make it a pleasure for me. Unamuno was a leftist novelist, playwright, and essayist who served as a professor of Greek and Classics at the University of Salamanca. Written in 1907 and published in 1914, “Niebla” (translated as mist or fog) purports through one of its characters to represent a new form of fiction, called the “nivola”, which is concerned more with the personification of ideas than realism. It plays on proto-existentialist concepts such as the absurdity of the illusion of free will, the Shakespearean sense of our life as being like actors on a stage, and more ancient notions of our existence being but a dream by our God. The special and delightful twist comes when our main character, Augusto, recognizes Unamumo himself as the god-author responsible for his torments in seeking love. Thus, we get a foretaste of the over-the-top shenanigans I recently read in Pirandello’s play “Six Characters in Search of an Author” (published the same year as Ulysses, 1922), which in turn presages the “Theater of the Absurd”, which Beckett initiated in the 50s, as well as the intersections of characters, readers, and authors seen in Calvino’s “If on a winter’s night a traveler” (1979). <br /><br />Recognizing this book as innovative does not prove it to be a fun read for contemporary readers. Let me paint the scenario a bit to help you see if it could be your cup of tea. We meet Augusto who is an aimless aristocrat. Strike one against our loving him. But he begins to charm us with his innocence and naivete and can readily appreciate his impetus to seek love to fill some of the emptiness he feels in life. A random encounter and glance with a woman on the street, Eugenia, leads him to follow her and then to dig information about her from the concierge at her residence. Strike two against him for stalking. But as he convinces himself he is in love with her and persuades her guardian aunt and uncle (a “theoretical anarchist”) of the virtues of his intentions, we begin to root for him much as we would for the fantasies of Don Quixote over Dulcinea. A windmill he must tilt at is that Eugenia is already engaged to marry. Maybe he is deluded, but his eloquence can be fetching:<br /><br /><i>Thanks to love, I can feel my soul in a palpable way, I can touch it. The very core of my soul aches, thanks to love. And what is the soul if not love, if not the incarnation of pain?<br />…Days come and go and love remains. Deep within, in the innermost depths, this world’s current rubs and grates against the other world’s current, and from this rubbing and grating comes the saddest and sweetest heartache of all, the heartache of living.</i><br /><br />As Augusto recognizes that only virtue can win the day for him, he bides his time while demonstrating selfless generosities to Eugenia. Meanwhile, the love awakened in his heart opens him to the susceptibility of falling in love with other women he encounters. The laundry girl, Rosario, catches his eye, and soon the dance of affection ensues between them. In his egocentric state of loving two women, Augusto seeks out advice from his writer friend Victor, framed as a quest to understand “feminine psychology.” He fills Augusto’s head with the notion from some 17th century doctor that women share a communal soul, that love is all in his head (“how do you know you are really in love and not just that you think you are?”), and that he should commit himself to the experiment of marrying either one immediately. To Augusto’s objection about the permanence of marriage, Victor is dismissive: <i>Anybody who wants to run an experiment with a way out, without burning bridges, will never attain any real knowledge.</i><br /><br />I found myself engaged in a hit at the plate beating a strike-three count. What would be Augusto’s just fate? Just as he is about to take his fate into his own hands, we get the absurdity of him taking his case to the author. But the reader gets the last laugh. Victor has set up the idea of the reader’s power in this earlier dialogue with Augusto:<br /><i>“All right, but what am I going to do right now?”<br />“Do…do…do! You’re already feeling like a character in a play or novel. …Aren’t we doing something when we talk like this? …As if talk were not action. In the beginning was the Word, and through the Word everything was created.”<br />…” The soul of a character in a play or a novel, or a nivola, is given to him by …”<br />“By his author.”<br />“No, by the reader.”</i><br /><br />A charming dessert at the end is an internal soliloquy by Augusto’s dog, Orfeo, who has had to put up with his master’s endless blather for so long. As a sliver of a sample of this perfect end to the tale, Orfeo concludes:<br /><i> Man is such a strange animal. …There is no way of knowing what he wants—if he himself even knows. …Language enables him to lie, to invent what doesn’t exist and get confused. …Man is a sick animal, no doubt about it.</i><br /><br />This book was provided by the publisher for review through the Netgalley program.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5732033974895931688" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1983197122518058077">I got my paws on an English translation of NIEBLA by Miguel de Unamuno. It was written over 100 years ago & yet it reeks of postmodern metafiction: you know, the type in which a character finds himself literally becoming invented or destroyed by his god, our author. It is an important feat, a relevant historical artifact in how the writer tries to break ties with classic conventions of the novel. "We exist, we exist!" is the cry of these malcontents, these fictional persons. But read now, in the</span>
  <span id="freeText1983197122518058077" style="display:none">I got my paws on an English translation of NIEBLA by Miguel de Unamuno. It was written over 100 years ago &amp; yet it reeks of postmodern metafiction: you know, the type in which a character finds himself literally becoming invented or destroyed by his god, our author. It is an important feat, a relevant historical artifact in how the writer tries to break ties with classic conventions of the novel. "We exist, we exist!" is the cry of these malcontents, these fictional persons. But read now, in the middle of the #MeToo movement, it could be a wee unsavory, as Unamuno traverses the societal &amp; personal reasons for dating women. In the 1910's.<br /><br />And how could "Fog" not be this way, when the author himself alludes to THE premier Spanish work, obviously DON QUIXOTE. This is an extension of that very Spanish trait--the one in which the writer cannot unglue himself from his creation, indeed intrinsically becoming part of the work himself.</span>

<div id="review_1571427042" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1571427042" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=novels-novellas-short-stories">novels-novellas-short-stories</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1571427042" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15770640407950717020">I had such a positive experience with my first Spanish review that I'm attempting another one. Once again, if anyone notices any errors, don't hesitate to tell me! I'm still a big <i>guiri</i>. (The translation is in the spoiler below.)<br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>La palabra, este producto social, se ha hecho para mentir.</i>
</blockquote><br/>Recuerdo cuando visité la Universidad de Salamanca. Es una de las universidades más viejas en Europa que todavía está abierta. La fachada del edificio de las escuelas mayores es un ejemplo glorioso del estilo </span>
  <span id="freeText15770640407950717020" style="display:none">I had such a positive experience with my first Spanish review that I'm attempting another one. Once again, if anyone notices any errors, don't hesitate to tell me! I'm still a big <i>guiri</i>. (The translation is in the spoiler below.)<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>La palabra, este producto social, se ha hecho para mentir.</i>
</blockquote><br />Recuerdo cuando visité la Universidad de Salamanca. Es una de las universidades más viejas en Europa que todavía está abierta. La fachada del edificio de las escuelas mayores es un ejemplo glorioso del estilo plateresco. Dentro hay mapas históricos, una biblioteca maravillosa, y varias salas de lectura. Una de estas salas está dedicada a Miguel de Unamuno, profesor, filósofo, y escritor. Durante el régimen de Franco, no podrías encontrar el nombre de Unamuno allí, porque él estuvo en contra del dictador. Pero ahora tiene la dedicación del que se merece.<br /><br /><i>Niebla</i> es uno de sus libros más famosos. Es un ejemplo temprano de la novela modernista. Pero es muy diferente de otros ejemplos que conozco. Su lenguaje es muy simple y directo. La mayoría del libro es diálogo, por consiguiente la experiencia de leerla es como la experiencia de leer una obra de teatro. El resto es un monólogo interior de la protagonista. La idea es permitir que los personajes se escriban. Se escriben por conversación, y con frecuencia esta conversación es desorganizada e inconsecuente.<br /><br />El argumento es un poco de un telenovela, con un montón de dramatismo tonto. Pero también hay preguntas filosóficas sobre la relación entre ficción y realidad, en concreto la relación entre un autor y sus personajes. Esta cuestión también está en la obra más famosa de literatura española: <i>Don Quijote</i>. Sin duda, esto es intencional. Cervantes tiene una presencia grande en estas paginas; él era obviamente un héroe de Unamuno. Y en mi opinión, <i>Niebla</i> es un sucesor digno de la obra maestra de Cervantes; es graciosa, inteligente, y original. Los personajes son memorable, y el fin es estupendo. Ahora tengo ganas a leer una obra filosófica de Unamuno, específicamente su libro <i>Del sentimiento trágico de la vida</i>. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I remember when I visited the University of Salamanca. It is one of the oldest universities in Europe that’s still open. The façade of the Escuela Mayores building is a glorious example of the plateresque style. Inside are historical maps, a marvellous library, and various lecture rooms. One of these rooms is dedicated to Miguel de Unamuno, professor, philosopher, and writer. During Franco’s reign, you couldn’t find Unamuno’s name there, because he was opposed to the dictator. But now he has the dedication he deserves.<br /><br /><i>Niebla</i> is one of his most famous books. It is an early example of the modernist novel. But it is very different from other examples I know. His language is very simple and straightforward. The majority of the book is dialogue, and consequently the experience of reading it is like the experience of reading a play. The rest is an interior monologue of the protagonist. The idea is to allow the characters to write themselves. They write themselves through conversation, and frequently the conversation is disorganized and inconsequential.<br /><br />The plot is a bit like a soap opera, with a lot of silly drama. But there are also philosophical questions about the relationship between fiction and reality, more precisely the relationship between the author and his characters. This question is also addressed in the most famous work of Spanish literature: <i>Don Quixote</i>. Without doubt, this is intentional. Cervantes has a big presence in these pages; he was obviously a hero of Unamuno. And in my opinion <i>Niebla</i> is a worth succesor to Cervantes’s masterpiece; it is funny, intelligent, and original. The characters are memorable, and the ending is terrific. Now I’m excited to read a philosophical work by Unamuno, specifically his <i>Tragic Sense of Life</i>. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15770640407950717020" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2006073538" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2006073538" />
    <a title="Joseph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5860412-joseph"><img alt="Joseph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549200708p2/5860412.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2006073538?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joseph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joseph" href="/user/show/5860412-joseph">Joseph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810135369" href="/book/show/32072611-fog">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=20th-century-fiction">20th-century-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=fiction-in-translation">fiction-in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5860412-joseph?shelf=humour-and-satire">humour-and-satire</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2006073538" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11290254366882234712">In his introduction to this English edition of Miguel de Unamuno’s Niebla (“Mist” or, as in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15816815.Elena_Barcia' title='Elena Barcia' rel='nofollow noopener'>Elena Barcia</a>’s new translation – “Fog”), <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3602.Alberto_Manguel' title='Alberto Manguel' rel='nofollow noopener'>Alberto Manguel</a> makes a bold claim for the novel. Critics, he tells us, have almost unanimously placed it amongst the great Modernist texts, next to Virginia Woolf’s The Waves and Pirandello’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11483158.Sei_personaggi_in_cerca_d_autore' title='Sei personaggi in cerca d'autore by Luigi Pirandello' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sei personaggi in cerca d'autore</a>. Except that Unamuno’s novel precedes them both, having been published in 1914 and commenced years before. <br/><br/>Now I have a confession to make. A</span>
  <span id="freeText11290254366882234712" style="display:none">In his introduction to this English edition of Miguel de Unamuno’s Niebla (“Mist” or, as in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15816815.Elena_Barcia" title="Elena Barcia" rel="nofollow noopener">Elena Barcia</a>’s new translation – “Fog”), <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3602.Alberto_Manguel" title="Alberto Manguel" rel="nofollow noopener">Alberto Manguel</a> makes a bold claim for the novel. Critics, he tells us, have almost unanimously placed it amongst the great Modernist texts, next to Virginia Woolf’s The Waves and Pirandello’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11483158.Sei_personaggi_in_cerca_d_autore" title="Sei personaggi in cerca d'autore by Luigi Pirandello" rel="nofollow noopener">Sei personaggi in cerca d'autore</a>. Except that Unamuno’s novel precedes them both, having been published in 1914 and commenced years before. <br /><br />Now I have a confession to make. Although a fan of Italian literature, I have never read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7702.Luigi_Pirandello" title="Luigi Pirandello" rel="nofollow noopener">Luigi Pirandello</a>, mainly because I have always been afraid that my tastes are too traditional to appreciate this experimental master. As for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/46114.The_Waves" title="The Waves by Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">The Waves</a> – I did read the novel over twenty years ago, but that was only because it was lent to me by a girl I fancied. And if the rocker Meat Loaf sang that he “would do anything for Love”, I guessed that having a go at Woolf was no big deal. Alas, The Waves washed over me without leaving any long-lasting ripples and I’ve never felt any inclination to tackle Woolf since then. It was therefore with some trepidation that I approached Unamuno’s book. I needn’t have worried, as the novel turned out to be really fun to read. And by “fun” I do not simply mean that it is “interesting” and “intellectually satisfying” (although it is that is well) but it is also seriously entertaining. <br /><br />As in any self-respecting Modernist novel, the plot is secondary, if not inexistent. Bored bachelor Augusto Pérez has lost his doting mother who, before passing on, insists that he find himself a wife. It takes the gaze of piano-teacher Eugenia to finally awake Augusto’s passions. There is a problem though - the wilful Eugenia is not particularly drawn to Augusto. Apart from the fact that she already has a fiancé. Moreover, thanks to Eugenia, Augusto’s eyes are finally open to the charms of women in general, and the ones who surround him in particular. Meaning that he is soon embroiled in a nascent affair with the earthier Rosario, the young woman who does his laundry. In between Augusto’s hapless attempts at lovemaking, he indulges in philosophical discussions and meta-fictional discourses with the other characters, which culminate in a showdown with the Author himself. Add a prologue purportedly written by one of Unamuno’s fictional characters, a “postprologue” by the author, and an epilogue by Augusto’s dog, and you have the makings of a Modernist text, a work which challenges preconceptions about the role of the author, his characters and his readers. <br /><br />What is surprising is that even at his most abstruse, Unamuno retains a light and comic touch. Indeed, when not exploding novelistic conventions to smithereens, he indulges in a type of comedy which reminds me of early <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11315.Evelyn_Waugh" title="Evelyn Waugh" rel="nofollow noopener">Evelyn Waugh</a>. I particularly enjoyed the scenes involving Eugenia’s uncle - a self-declared “theoretical, mystical anarchist” who believes that Esperanto will bring about world peace. <br /><br />I sincerely hope that Elena Barcia’s translation will bring this novel to the attention of a wider English-speaking (and reading) public. It deserves to be known not only for its literary-historical merits, but also – and perhaps more importantly – because it is such a great read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11290254366882234712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_612837755" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/612837755" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45836-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1421786395p2/45836.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/612837755?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/45836-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Pasta blanda 9788437603476" href="/book/show/45673.Niebla">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45836-andrew?shelf=literature-classic">literature-classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer612837755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16161218977111980302">This was a very slow, difficult and tedious read that surged in quality at the end after the author inserted himself into the story. Though there are some interesting metaphysical discussions, almost nothing happens for the first 3/4 of the novel; all we have is a bored playboy pining for one or two different women and talking to people (mostly himself) about it. (<b>Caveat</b>: I read this book in Spanish and while I'm fluent and understood over 95% of the book, the energy needed to understand a non-n</span>
  <span id="freeText16161218977111980302" style="display:none">This was a very slow, difficult and tedious read that surged in quality at the end after the author inserted himself into the story. Though there are some interesting metaphysical discussions, almost nothing happens for the first 3/4 of the novel; all we have is a bored playboy pining for one or two different women and talking to people (mostly himself) about it. (<b>Caveat</b>: I read this book in Spanish and while I'm fluent and understood over 95% of the book, the energy needed to understand a non-native language surely factored into my feeling of tediousness. I have little doubt that I would have enjoyed it more in English.)<br /><br />I believe I understand what Unamuno was trying to do to the format by creating a <i>nivola</i> instead of a <i>novela</i> (Span.). His surrogate character explains, "What we have is dialogue; above all, dialogue. The thing is that the characters talk, they talk a lot, although they say nothing. . . because people like conversation in itself, even though nothing is said." Toward the end, when the anguished Augusto asks "What am I going to do now?" Victor responds (my translation):<blockquote>Do, do, do! Bah, you're acting like a character in a drama or novel! Let's content ourselves with being those of a. . . <i>nivola</i>! Do. . . do. . . do! Does it seem to you that we're not doing enough by talking like this? It's the mania of action, or in other words, of pantomime. They say that many things occur in a drama when the actors can make many gestures and take giant steps and fake duels and jump and. . . Pantomime! Pantomime! Other times it's said, 'They talk too much!' As if talking weren't doing. In the beginning was the Word and by the Word everything was made. . .</blockquote>This passage and those around it are excellent descriptions of what you're in for if you pick up this book. I like this concept and I agree that conversation and discourse have been devalued (even moreso almost 100 years after this book was published). However, that doesn't automatically make the embodiment of these ideas into a compelling reading experience, and this one struggles on that level. To be sure there are also cultural and linguistic differences at play here. Hispanic language and culture value the circuitous in speaking and writing, which can be a difficult and frustrating adjustment for us Anglos.<br /><br />Probably the most famous part of the book is when Unamuno himself comes in and really spices things up. He is able to discuss directly with his protagonist the nature and value of existence, fictional and otherwise. Augusto raises an excellent point that he, in fact, is more real than Unamuno since he will last longer as a fictional entity than Unamuno will in history. I love these ideas, although again, reading them in a novel is not very engaging. <br /><br />Ultimately, however, I am very happy to have experienced the book and recommend other students of literature -- whether professional or amateur -- do the same. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/770305.Abel_S_nchez_and_Other_Stories" title="Abel Sánchez and Other Stories by Miguel de Unamuno" rel="nofollow noopener">Abel Sanchez and Other Stories</a>, which I read just before this, was both more entertaining and less thought-provoking (although it's amusing to go back and see that my principle critique of "Abel" was that it was too talky). <br /><br />Still, the most impressive aspect of Unamuno remains the quote that first brought his name to my attention, when in 1936 he replied to a fascist speech by General Millán-Astray at the university where he would later have to resign. This is what he said:<blockquote>You are waiting for my words. You know me well, and know I cannot remain silent for long. Sometimes, to remain silent is to lie, since silence can be interpreted as assent. . . But now I have heard this insensible and necrophilous oath, "¡Viva la Muerte!", and I, having spent my life writing paradoxes that have provoked the ire of those who do not understand what I have written, and being an expert in this matter, find this ridiculous paradox repellent. General Millán-Astray is a cripple. There is no need for us to say this with whispered tones. He is a war cripple. So was Cervantes. But unfortunately, Spain today has too many cripples. And, if God does not help us, soon it will have very many more. It torments me to think that General Millán-Astray could dictate the norms of the psychology of the masses. A cripple, who lacks the spiritual greatness of Cervantes, hopes to find relief by adding to the number of cripples around him. <br /><br />[Millán-Astray responded, "¡Muera la inteligencia! ¡Viva la Muerte!" ("Death to intelligence! Long live death!"), provoking applause from the Falangists.]<br /><br />[Unamuno continued] This is the temple of intelligence, and I am its high priest. You are profaning its sacred domain. You will win, because you have enough brute force. But you will not convince. In order to convince it is necessary to persuade, and to persuade you will need something that you lack: reason and right in the struggle. I see it is useless to ask you to think of Spain. I have spoken.</blockquote><br />He was escorted to safety by Franco's wife, and then removed from his post at the University of Salamanca. He died 10 weeks later.<br /><br />Thus anything I read by Unamuno will be colored by my knowledge that the man was a bonafide hero.<br /><br /><a href="http://notbadmoviereviews.wordpress.com/books/niebla" rel="nofollow noopener">Not Bad Reviews</a><br /><br /><a href="http://www.twitter.com/pointblaek" rel="nofollow noopener">@pointblaek</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="16161218977111980302" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2459350015" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2459350015" />
    <a title="Edward" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1659159-edward"><img alt="Edward" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308570322p2/1659159.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2459350015?book_show_action=true">Jul 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward" href="/user/show/1659159-edward">Edward</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780252068942" href="/book/show/144920.Mist">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2459350015" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14039128592581459028">What can you say about a book where the author decides to kill off his main character, but is met with a derisive comment that the victim will have the last laugh? Fictional characters, he says, once created, can't be discarded. They live on but their authors do in fact die, and their creations achieve immortality, or as close to it as anyone can come in life on earth. <br/>	<br/>You can hear the objection - fiction is not real life, just a imaginary, a make-believe one. That raises further questions abou</span>
  <span id="freeText14039128592581459028" style="display:none">What can you say about a book where the author decides to kill off his main character, but is met with a derisive comment that the victim will have the last laugh? Fictional characters, he says, once created, can't be discarded. They live on but their authors do in fact die, and their creations achieve immortality, or as close to it as anyone can come in life on earth. <br />	<br />You can hear the objection - fiction is not real life, just a imaginary, a make-believe one. That raises further questions about what is "real". Great joys and great pains, those events that make life meaningful "come wrapped in an immense MIST of trifling incidents. Life is just this - mist [often translated "fog"] Life is a nebula and out of it arises . . ." <br />	<br />Most of the time, though, "nearly all of us are boring ourselves unconsciously. Tedium is the foundation of life and it is tedium that invented all the games and distractions, the novels, and love." As if it were a wine press, "tedium" , squeezes from life a bitter-sweet liquor, any kind of creative endeavor, which in this novel turns out to be love. <br />	<br />What is depicted in this novel are the daily events of one Augusto who emerges from his personal "nebula" and falls in love with Eugenia. The "great joy" of his life is love, but it is indeed a bitter-sweet experience. Euigenia scorns him, thinking him a nincompoop, which he generally is. He reacts to Eugenia's rejection by finding another woman, but in the end returns to Eugenia who by now decided he is is not so bad after all. More surprises thwart a happy love ending., Augusto confides, discouraged, in his only true confident, Orpheo, his dog.<br />	<br />He wonders "how you can tell that a man is really in love and does not merely think it to be so?" Love's illusions are many, and a dog can't give answers. Here is where the author comes in; Augusto decides to talk over his dilemma with Unamuno who after all wrote the novel. Augusto, now despairing, , decides to commit suicide, but the author says he'll spare him the trouble and simply kill him off. At which point, Augusto shifts direction again and decides that he wants to live, after all, and in fact will live. He exists and the author, at this stage, simply can't remove him from existence. <br />	<br />It's finally unclear whether he killed himself or was technically "killed" by the author. All that's known is that the servant finds his lifeless body, and thinks she is going mad when Augusto speaks and tells her he can't be killed. A Dracula finale? Almost, with more talk that without the word, nothing exists (reminiscent of the beginning of John's gospel, "in the beginning was the word. . .").<br />	<br />An epilogue concludes this drolly surreal book with Augusto's faithful dog, Orfeo, having the last word, expressing that in a pure platonic world of ideas lives the idea of pure dog alongside his flawed master. You might consider this a philosophical book interrupted by the fog of daily life which has rolled in.. Or if you find all of this tedious, it could be a book of daily life, potentially a satisfying love story, which has been obscured by a dense fog of aesthetic and philosophical questions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14039128592581459028" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Anima" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59510944-anima"><img alt="Anima" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604460535p2/59510944.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

<div id="review_3633371471" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3633371471" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11970409-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494705351p2/11970409.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3633371471?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/11970409-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810135369" href="/book/show/32072611-fog">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11970409-tim?shelf=fiction-pre-1967">fiction-pre-1967</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3633371471" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4283265498454583728">This 2017 translation of Miguel de Unamuno’s “Niebla” or “Fog” was originally published in 1914 in Spanish. Top notch translation here. The words and phrasing feel modern, not like I imagine English speakers spoke in the early 20th century, but that doesn’t detract from this piece. Unamuno is considered a Modernist writer and this text is filled with questions psychological and existential on the nature of being, reality, truth and how those are perceived. We see each other and even ourselves th</span>
  <span id="freeText4283265498454583728" style="display:none">This 2017 translation of Miguel de Unamuno’s “Niebla” or “Fog” was originally published in 1914 in Spanish. Top notch translation here. The words and phrasing feel modern, not like I imagine English speakers spoke in the early 20th century, but that doesn’t detract from this piece. Unamuno is considered a Modernist writer and this text is filled with questions psychological and existential on the nature of being, reality, truth and how those are perceived. We see each other and even ourselves through a fog; perception is unclear. The framework for this is a rather simple love story (of course, the definition of love is questioned) with attendant drama and melodrama. The distinctly unmodern aspect of the story are some cringeworthy interactions between the male protagonist and women, which reek of outdated expectations of male/female behavior.<br /><br />I would recommend to most readers to not let that dissuade you from reading “Fog”. There’s a lot of grist for the 21st century thinker. To illustrate, here are some quotes I found relevant to contemporary topics.<br /><br /><b>On life during an epidemic</b><br />“Do you know what that boor Martín Rubio said to poor Don Emeterio a few days after he lost his wife?” <br /><br />“I don’t think I heard.” <br /><br />“Well, listen. It was during the epidemic, you know? Everybody was terrified. You wouldn’t let me out of the house for days, and I had to drink boiled water. Everyone was avoiding everyone else, and if you saw someone dressed in black, it was as if they had the plague. So, five or six days after losing his wife, poor Don Emeterio had to leave his house. He was dressed in mourning, of course, and he ran into that lout, Martín. <br /><br />When Martín saw that Don Emeterio was dressed in black, he stood a safe distance away, as if he was afraid he’d be infected and said, ‘What’s the matter? Has something happened at home?’ “‘Yes,’ poor Don Emeterio said, ‘I just lost my poor wife.’ “<br /><br /><b>On texting and written syntax</b><br />“Italics turn writers into mimes, replacing intensity and tone with gestures.”<br /><br /><b>On Climate Change Denial</b><br />“If some have mocked God, why shouldn’t we ridicule Reason, Science, and even Truth?”<br /><br /><b>On Gender Identity</b><br />“That’s why man invented clothes—to cover his sexual organs. But then, since both sexes wore the same clothes, they couldn’t tell each other apart. They couldn’t always tell what sex they were”…”At first, men and women wore the same garments, but since they couldn’t tell each other apart, they had to invent different clothing to reveal their gender.”<br /><br /><b>On state-sponsored socialism</b><br />“But as soon as Don Eloíno recovered from the shock, his friend made it clear that if he married the landlady, she would receive a widow’s pension of thirteen duros a month, which otherwise would be unclaimed and go to the state. So you see . . .” <br /><br />“Yes, Victor, I know more than one person who’s gotten married to prevent the state from keeping his pension. That’s civic-mindedness for you.”<br /><br /><b>Personally interesting to me were the explorations of inner voice, what the protagonist called monologues. Our internal thoughts, the voice in our head. Unamumo even touches on what we might label mindfulness.</b><br /><br />“Was there anything before books, before stories, before words, before thoughts?”<br /><br />“I can’t live without debate and contradictions. And when there’s no one to debate and contradict me, I invent someone inside me to do it. My monologues are all dialogues.”<br /><br />“As soon as we talk, we lie, and as soon as we talk to ourselves . . . I mean, as soon as we think, knowing that we’re thinking, we lie to ourselves. The only real truth is physiological. Words, these social constructs, were made for lying. In fact, I’ve heard one philosopher say that truth, like language, is also a social construct. Truth is what everybody believes, and in believing the same thing, we understand one another. Any social construct is a lie.”<br /><br />“When a man lying motionless and asleep in his bed dreams something, what exists more, he as a consciousness that dreams or his dream?”<br /><br />“…ridicule yourself, devour yourself. He who devours enjoys, but he worries so much about his pleasure coming to an end that he becomes a pessimist. He who is devoured suffers, but he focuses so much on the end of his torment that he becomes an optimist. Devour yourself, and since the pleasure of devouring yourself will become confused with and neutralized by the pain of being devoured, you’ll reach the state of perfect equanimity—ataraxy.”<br /><br /><b>(on receiving shocking news)</b><br />“He couldn’t think of anything to say. There was no monologue.”<br /><br /><b>(there is humor too)</b><br />“Don’t study medicine. It’s best not to know what’s inside you.”<br /><br /><b>And finally Orfeo, the dog, appropriately has the last word</b><br />“I taught him a lot with my silences, licking his hand while he talked and talked and talked.”</span>
  <a data-text-id="4283265498454583728" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1967956994" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1967956994" />
    <a title="Jonathan Bogart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3219427-jonathan-bogart"><img alt="Jonathan Bogart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1448770970p2/3219427.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1967956994?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonathan Bogart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonathan Bogart" href="/user/show/3219427-jonathan-bogart">Jonathan Bogart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780252068942" href="/book/show/144920.Mist">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3219427-jonathan-bogart?shelf=modernism-1890-1940">modernism-1890-1940</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3219427-jonathan-bogart?shelf=1910s-box">1910s-box</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1967956994" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer884707681364606032">To be reading this alongside <i>Zuleika Dobson</i> was a fascinating experience; I wonder whether anyone has ever thought to compare them before. They share many themes (romantic attraction, unpredictable women, suicide, metanarrative) and even a tone, saying baroquely funny things in a faux-ponderous manner. But Beerbohm has no serious point beneath his gossamer humor; and Unamuno, the philosopher of Spanish identity, does.<br/><br/>This novel -- or <i>nivola</i>, Unamuno's invented term for a nebulous narrative, more</span>
  <span id="freeText884707681364606032" style="display:none">To be reading this alongside <i>Zuleika Dobson</i> was a fascinating experience; I wonder whether anyone has ever thought to compare them before. They share many themes (romantic attraction, unpredictable women, suicide, metanarrative) and even a tone, saying baroquely funny things in a faux-ponderous manner. But Beerbohm has no serious point beneath his gossamer humor; and Unamuno, the philosopher of Spanish identity, does.<br /><br />This novel -- or <i>nivola</i>, Unamuno's invented term for a nebulous narrative, more philosophy than plot, and deconstructing the elements of fiction decades before deconstruction occurred to any French academic -- is probably the most celebrated Spanish novel of the 1910s; the only English translation dates from 1928, and is not entirely worthy of it. Although it can be read as an early metafictional novel in the sense popularized by Flann O'Brien, the <i>nouveau roman</i>, and the 1960s prankster postmodernists, Unamuno is really only following the lead of his teachers Cervantes and, to a lesser extent, Kierkegaard.<br /><br />As a philosopher he's no lightweight; but he's a really engaging storyteller as well, cutting description down to zero and letting his characters spin into baroquely philosophical and cod-philosophical dialogue. (Entirely typically, he inserts his reasons for doing so into the conversation; but what he doesn't mention is that he was also a successful playwright, and his conversations, when he gets around to dispensing with abstrusities or absurdities, have the narrative logic, the emotional force, and the sense of hinging as much on things unsaid as things said, as great dramatic dialogue.)<br /><br />This isn't the place for a close reading of my reasons for disagreeing with the back-cover copy and the academic introduction which calls the most prominent female character "deceitful, scheming," but although Unamuno was no feminist he wasn't the misogynist his political and ethnological conservatism might suggest. The central male character, the one who has all the philosophical discussions and experiences all the novel's Weltschmerz, is never not a figure of fun; the women are (for the most part) sensible, dispassionate, and self-respecting. But this is just a Goodreads blurb.<br /><br />Yeah, five stars. It's great.</span>
  <a data-text-id="884707681364606032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2973857430" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2973857430" />
    <a title="Kelly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1380226-kelly"><img alt="Kelly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575382684p2/1380226.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2973857430?book_show_action=true">Sep 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/1380226-kelly">Kelly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780810135369" href="/book/show/32072611-fog">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2973857430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14313779184138324454"><i>“‘I’m an idea.’ He began to devour the ham.”</i><br/><br/>Anyone who has a passing familiarity with literature of the early Twentieth Century will recognize the style, themes, and overall gist of <i>Fog</i>. It plays with its own self-awareness in a way that feels very specifically of-its-era. It’s a pleasure to read insofar as one enjoys relics from another time, but if your eyes tend to roll at yet <i>another</i> story of a man working out his existential issues through confused relationships with women—this one may not </span>
  <span id="freeText14313779184138324454" style="display:none"><i>“‘I’m an idea.’ He began to devour the ham.”</i><br /><br />Anyone who has a passing familiarity with literature of the early Twentieth Century will recognize the style, themes, and overall gist of <i>Fog</i>. It plays with its own self-awareness in a way that feels very specifically of-its-era. It’s a pleasure to read insofar as one enjoys relics from another time, but if your eyes tend to roll at yet <i>another</i> story of a man working out his existential issues through confused relationships with women—this one may not be for you.<br /><br />There is apparently a host of philosophical and theological themes underpinning the narrative, but they’re not particularly burdensome on the reader. Mostly, the tone is mildly satirical and perhaps a little cheeky (not unlike Pirandello, for those familiar with <i>Six Characters in Search of an Author</i>). It's a decent little book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14313779184138324454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer219498892" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9605350687602239328">What a peculiar little book! I read it about five years ago and find it way easier to understand this second time. It's very philosophical, full of musings and monologues and, may I say, free association. Some of the subjects mentioned are love, death, divinity, existence and reality. The author shakes and eventually smashes the fourth wall, providing strong arguments about reality and fiction, sanity and madness, clarity and mist.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18701990-jeremy?shelf=mod-ism">mod-ism</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2517201340" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1180560911131482178">Machado de Assis, Vonnegut's Brazilian great grandfather, is also father to Unamuno in his "nivola" mode. But it's Cervantes, in body and spirit, that looms largest here; Unamuno jousts with Quijote to explore the intersection of identity and imagination, in a way that reminds me of Joyce vs Hamlet, only with less contention, more affection. Fun and funny, and Elena Barcia is a pitch-perfect englisher. Here's to more Unamuno from Northwestern.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13320546298785215672">There are few words that can explain the jumble that the brain is in once you finish this book. Unamuno's own internal battle with creation and will surfaces in this fascinating piece, and in one of the most famous literary scenes every written, in which the main character confronts his author, Unamuno, himself. I think the English title is "Mist," although Niebla can also mean "Fog."</span>

          </span>
        </div>

The hole no</span>
  <span id="freeText14309667313112057369" style="display:none">At first, you think you are reading the story of a young man and his discovery of women, but when you are getting kind of bored with this overwhelmed protagonist you discover it's all an excuse of the author to build this experimental "nivola" (he doesn't name it novel as he jokes with inventing a new genre with his own rules).<br />As this man goes crazy for Eugenia, he will see himself "reborn" in terms of discovering the nature and charm of women as much as the joy and sorrow of "love". The hole novel has an ironic style as it's headed to ridiculize social conventions. The old-fashioned mindset is all over the place as MARRIAGE seems to be the ultimate solution regardless true feelings. The women around Augusto will know exactly how to play this game, showing themselves at times vulnerable and reachable and at other times way ahead of the young man's intentions and plans.<br />This is the ultimate parody of cultural precepts such as "love", family, parenthood and genre roles. But as the character reaches darker and deeper knowledge of his motivations and aspirations, he will make you face philosophical matters (expected in a novel of de Unamuno) such as embracing mortality or searching the nature of self awareness</span>
  <a data-text-id="14309667313112057369" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText3163630317474201581" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>"'When you hear that a drama is full of action it means that the actors are indulging in a host of gestures, performing mighty deeds, fighting stage duels, skipping about and — pantomime! Pantomime! Or you may hear that there is too much talk in the play. As if talk were not action! In the beginning was the Word, and by the Word all things were made. Suppose, for example, that some — some 'nivolist' were hiding here now, back of that wardrobe, and taking stenographic notes of all that we are saying — suppose that he should reproduce them, very likely his readers would say that there is nothing happening here; and yet —'<br /><br />'Ah, Victor, they wouldn't say that if they could see inside!'<br /><br />'Inside? Inside whom? You? Me? We haven't any inside. The time when they would not say that nothing is happening here, would be when they could see the inside of themselves — of themselves, the readers. The soul of a character in a play, in a novel, or in a </i>nivola<i>, has no inside except that of the —'<br /><br />'Yes, of the author.'<br /><br />'No, of the reader.'"</i> (287)</blockquote></span>

<div id="review_32991403" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/32991403" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/32991403?book_show_action=true">Sep 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janshrmn1" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janshrmn1" href="/user/show/1533733-janshrmn1">Janshrmn1</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>
